Lending Club Loan Application analysis[¶](#Lending-Club-Loan-Application-analysis)
==================================================================================

In \[1\]:

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import seaborn as sns

The natural statistical analysis to do on a loan application data is predicting loan default. However, considering that there is considerable literature concerning this subject, we wanted to explore another interest aspect of loan application, namely discrimination exhibited in the applications. The question that we will be addressing is the following:

### Assuming that the demographic information can be inferred from a borrower’s home state, can we identify a bias (whether positive or negative) in loan statistics towards a particular group of borrowers?[¶](#Assuming-that-the-demographic-information-can-be-inferred-from-a-borrower%E2%80%99s-home-state,-can-we-identify-a-bias-(whether-positive-or-negative)-in-loan-statistics-towards-a-particular-group-of-borrowers?)

Lending Club uses FICO scores and applicant screening to determine their credit grades and sub grades. This is then used to calculate an interest rate based on that grade and various borrower characteristics. One could wonder if there are any other factors included in establishing these grades. Applicants must declare their postal codes when applying for a loan. It is entirely possible for applicants that live in less favourable states or areas to receive lower credit grades and hence higher interest rates, denial rates, or changed hardship loan characteristics as well.

Contents[¶](#Contents)
----------------------

Non-Technical Executive Summary[¶](#Non-Technical-Executive-Summary)
--------------------------------------------------------------------

There are 2 main forms of discrimination to look at in order to answer our problem statement:

1.  Taste-based discrimination

This was introduced by Becker in his 1957 book "The economics of discrimination". In this model, employers hold a ‘taste for discrimination,’ meaning that there is a disamenity value to employing minority workers. Hence, minority workers may have to ‘compensate’ employers by being more productive at a given wage or, equivalently, by accepting a lower wage for identical productivity. (Autor, 2003)

This form of discrimination is not truly possible for us to addresss using only the data about the state that an individual lives in without making a number of unrealistic assumptions. Thus, we must focus on another type of discrimination.

1.  Statistical discrimination

Statistical discrimination occurs when distinctions between demographic groups are made on the basis of real or imagined statistical distinctions between the groups. While such discrimination is legal in some cases (e.g., insurance markets), it is illegal and/or controversial in others (e.g., racial profiling and gender-based labor market discrimination). "First moment" statistical discrimination occurs when, for example, female workers are offered lower wages because females are perceived to be less productive, on average, than male workers.

This can indeed to some extent be inferred from our data as we can statewide statistics to put states into different groups and hence all applicants into different groups to understand whether there is any varied treatment between those groups.

Loan Denial analysis[¶](#Loan-Denial-analysis)
----------------------------------------------

We used US census data statistics by state (health, crime rates, unemployment rates, GDP per capita, educational attainment) in order to rank the states. In order to capture any year by year changes, the ranking was done for each year from 2014 to 2018, the timeframe within which all provided data falls.

We then apply these rankings to the loan denial data with around 2 milllion accepted applications and 26 milllion denied applications.

We can observe the following when plotting bar charts for each of the 3 state groups and their loan denial rates:

In \[3\]:

loan\_denials\=pd.read\_csv('loan\_denials\_all.csv')
years\=\[2014, 2015, 2016, 2017, 2018\]
for year in years:
    #count number of accepted loans by Loan\_Title
    denials\_year\=loan\_denials\[loan\_denials\['Year'\]\==year\]
    total\_cat\_count \= denials\_year.groupby('State\_Category').count()
    accepted\_cat\_count\=denials\_year\[\['State\_Category', 'Accepted'\]\].groupby('State\_Category').sum()
    frac\_accepted\_cat\_count\=(accepted\_cat\_count/total\_cat\_count)\['Accepted'\]

    #plot the fraction of accepted loans by Loan\_Title
    plt.figure(figsize\=(10,5))
    plt.bar(frac\_accepted\_cat\_count.index,frac\_accepted\_cat\_count)
    plt.title('Fraction of Accepted Loans by State Category in '+f'{year}')
    #angle the x-axis labels
    plt.xlabel('State Category')
    plt.xticks(rotation\=90)
    plt.ylabel('Fraction of Accepted Loans in '+f'{year}')
    plt.show()

We can see that the fraction of accepted loans by state category is generally decreasing giving us a broad suggestion that a person's state category does indeed matter in determining their loan acceptance. Then after data cleaning we can extract the following correlation heatmap for the features in the loan denial data (with 'Accepted' as the target variable):

)

The correlation heatmap shows that the features are not correlated with each other in general, but on the downside, we can see that the State\_Category columns are also not strongly correlated with the target variable. If we extract the extact figures, they are the following:

*   For the State\_Category\_2 column, the correlation is -0.011320
*   For the State\_Category\_3 column, the correlation coefficient is -0.019901.

Although this seems reassuring in terms of the numbers being negative, we can also see that the State\_Category column are not well correlated with the target variable. Nonetheless, we can look at the coefficients for the State\_Category columns when we run a standard logistic regression on them.

The reason logistic regression was chosen as the baseline is that loan denial is a binary classification problem, to which a logistic curve tends to fit much better as it bounds the values between 0 and 1 and emulates a step function quite well.

### Logistic Regression Loan Denials[¶](#Logistic-Regression-Loan-Denials)

The coefficients when running the logistic regression came out as follows:

The coefficients of -0.87218 for the State\_Category\_2 column and -0.794822 for the State\_Category\_3 column do initially seem to point towards high levels of statistical discrimination. However, this relationship cannot be assumed to be causal and could simply be the product of the lower numbered state category having objectively stronger applications.

The AUC score of the logistic regression model is 0.68, on both the training and test sets, which although means that there was minimal overfitting, the score is still rather low. Thus, it is clearly not the optimal model to use to conclude on discrimination in loan denials. The confusion matrix for the logistic regression model is:

)

The confusion matrix shows that although there is a very high accuracy in predicting denials at 0.98, there is a relatively low accuracy in predicting accepts at 0.7. This is could be due to the model not being able to handle the imbalance in the data in terms of only having 2 million acceptances and 26 million denials.

### Random Forest Loan Denials[¶](#Random-Forest-Loan-Denials)

We then run a random forest with mainly standard hyperparameters on the loan denial data. The only changes made was to change the number of trees to 10 and the max depth to 20. The max depth being low means that the likelihood of overfitting is low. The number of trees being low similarly means that the model is more likely to be able to generalize to new data.

The AUC score of the random forest model is 0.84, on both the training and test sets, which means that there was minimal overfitting, and the score is quite high. Thus, it is a better model to investigate the influence of the State\_Category columns on loan denials. To do this, we can look for the feature importances of the random forest model.

Feature importances are a way to quantify the importance of a feature in a model. The higher the importance, the more important the feature is. The feature importances are calculated by taking the average of the feature importance of each tree in the forest. The feature importance of a feature is the average of the decrease in the loss function for a tree when that feature is removed. These importances can be seen below:

In \[7\]:

Image(filename\='rf\_loan\_denials\_feature\_importances.png')

)

Thus, the State\_Category columns are not at all important features in the model, which shows that although there seems to be a clear downward trend with state category, the models both do not suggest that the State\_Category columns are a good predictor of loan denials. Thus, we can conclude there is no direct causation between the State\_Category columns and loan denials. This implies we cannot conlude with confidence that there exists any inherent statistical bias.

Interest Rate Analysis[¶](#Interest-Rate-Analysis)
--------------------------------------------------

After looking at both denied and accepted loans, it is now time to look at the loan terms given to loans that have been accepted. We now look at the coefficients from the linear regression with interest rate as dependent variable. Using similar features we first investigate using a linear regression model as this data is continuous.

)

The correlation heatmap shows that the features are not correlated with each other in general, but on the downside, we can see that the State\_Category columns are also not strongly correlated with the target variable. If we extract the extact figures, they are the following:

*   For the State\_Category\_2 column, the correlation is 0.011
*   For the State\_Category\_3 column, the correlation coefficient is 0.014

Although this seems reassuring in terms of the numbers being positive, we can also see that the State\_Category column are not well correlated with the target variable. Nonetheless, we can look at the coefficients for the State\_Category columns when we run a standard linear regression on them.

In \[9\]:

coefs\_linear\=pd.read\_csv('coefficients.csv')

The coefficients of -0.06 for the State\_Category\_2 column and 0.030264 for the State\_Category\_3 column do initially seem to point towards very little statistical discrimination.

The r2 score of the linear regression model is 0.1, on both the training and test sets, which means that there was minimal overfitting, and the score is extremely low. Thus, it is a better model to investigate the influence of the State\_Category columns on loan denials. To do this, we can look at a random forest model with the same parameters as the linear regression model.

### Random Forest Interest Rate[¶](#Random-Forest-Interest-Rate)

We then run a random forest with mainly standard hyperparameters on the loan denial data. The only changes made was to change the number of trees to 10 and the max depth to 20. The max depth being low means that the likelihood of overfitting is low. The number of trees being low similarly means that the model is more likely to be able to generalize to new data.

The r2 value is higher around 0.15, on both the training and test sets, which means that there was minimal overfitting, and the score is quite high. Thus, it is a better model to investigate the influence of the State\_Category columns on loan denials. To do this, we can look at the feature importances of the random forest model.

Io8wAAAACgQvwPJlruoVWsJCEAAAAASUVORK5CYII=
)

Thus, the State\_Category columns are more important features in the model than for loan denials, which shows that there seems to be a clear downward trend with state category, and the models do to some degree suggest that the State\_Category columns are a good predictor of interest rates. Thus, we can conclude there is some level of causation between the State\_Category columns and interest rates.
